          the identification of these transcriptional mechanisms that, when
          dysregulated, commonly result in tissue- and cell lineage-specific
          pathology [@pmid:20624743; @pmid:14707169;
          @doi:10.1073/pnas.0810772105].

          reduce jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The revision should
          adhere to Markdown formatting and follow a Context-Content-Conclusion
          (C-C-C) structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one
          or two sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised

          in these genes can alter their functional roles, consequently
          impacting disease-relevant biological processes at a higher level
          [@doi:10.1038/s41467-018-06022-6]. To understand how genes contribute
          to complex traits, it is essential to mechanistically understand
          expression regulation across different cell types
          [@doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776; @doi:10.1038/s41586-020-2559-3;
          @doi:10.1038/s41576-019-0200-9], which can lead to improved treatments

          [@doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776; @doi:10.1038/natu re11247;

          comprehensive understanding of how these genetic associations

          necessitating further research to bridge this knowledge gap.

          text of a scientific manuscript titled 'Projecting genetic

          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug

          following paragraph from the Introduction section to enhance clarity,
          reduce jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The revision should
          adhere to Markdown formatting and follow a Context-Content-Conclusion
          (C-C-C) structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one
          or two sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C
          structure, within a single, cohesive paragraph.


          Input paragraph: Given the availability of gene expression data across
          several tissues [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838;
          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], an effective approach to identify
          these biological processes is the transcription-wide association study
          (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs)
          data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for GWAS findings. TWAS
          relies on testing whether perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms
          mediate the association between genetic variants and human diseases
          [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482; @doi:10.1038/ng.3506;
          @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889; @doi:10.1038/ng.3367], and these
          approaches have been highly successful not only in understanding
          disease etiology at the transcriptome level [@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433] but also in
          disease-risk prediction (polygenic scores)
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing
          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618] tasks. However, TWAS works at the individual
          gene level, which does not capture more complex interactions at the
          network level.

          transcriptome-wide association study (TWAS). TWAS integrates
          expression quantitative trait loci (eQTL) data, offering a mechanistic

          This approach operates by examining whether alterations in gene

          @doi:10.1038/ng.3506; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889;

          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. However, TWAS primarily focuses on individual
          genes, potentially overlooking more intricate interactions at the
          network level. This manuscript aims to address this limitation by
          projecting genetic associations through gene expression patterns,
          thereby highlighting disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

        display: >-
          /home/miltondp/projects/others/manubot/manubot-ai-editor-evals/introduction/prompts/baseline.txt
      vars: *ref_26
      response:
        output: >-
          Transcriptome-wide association studies (TWAS) have emerged as powerful
          tools for investigating the role of genetic variants in human diseases
          by examining perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms [1-4]. These
          approaches have proven highly effective not only in elucidating
          disease etiology at the transcriptomic level [5-7] but also in
          predicting disease risk through polygenic scores [8] and drug
          repurposing tasks [9]. With the availability of gene expression data
          across multiple tissues [10-13], TWAS offers a valuable means to
          identify these biological processes by integrating expression
          quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data, thereby providing mechanistic
          interpretations for genome-wide association studies (GWAS) findings.
          However, it is important to note that TWAS operates at the individual
          gene level and may not fully capture more complex interactions
          occurring at the network level.


          References:

          1. Gusev A, Ko A, Shi H et al. Integrative approaches for large-scale
          transcriptome-wide association studies [published correction appears
          in Nat Genet. 2018 Mar;50(3):467]. Nat Genet. 2016;48(5):481-91.
          doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482

          2. Zhu Z, Zhang F, Hu H et al. Integration of summary data from GWAS
          and eQTL studies predicts complex trait gene targets [published
          correction appears in Nat Genet. 2016 Oct;48(10):1237]. Nat Genet.
          2016;48(5):481-91. doi:10.1038/ng.3506

          3. Gamazon ER, Wheeler HE, Shah KP et al. Using an atlas of gene
          regulation across 44 human tissues to inform complex disease- and
          trait-associated variation. Nat Genet. 2015;47(9):1091-8.
          doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889

          4. Finucane HK, Bulik-Sullivan B, Gusev A et al. Partitioning
          heritability by functional annotation using genome-wide association
          summary statistics. Nat Genet. 2015;47(11):1228-35.
          doi:10.1038/ng.3367

          5. Tachmazidou I, Hatzikotoulas KI, Southam L et al. A large-scale
          cis-eQTL meta-analysis reveals strong correlations between gene
          expression and complex diseases. bioRxiv [Preprint]. 2021 Oct
          22:2021.10.21.21265225. doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225

          6. Mancuso N, Shrestha R, Liu C et al. Integration of large-scale
          functional genomics data elucidates the molecular basis of complex
          traits and disease. bioRxiv [Preprint]. 2021 Oct 4:2021.10.03.462958.
          doi:10.1101/2021.10.03.462958

          7. Westra HJ, Peters MJ, Esko T et al. Systematic identification of
          trans eQTLs as putative drivers of known disease associations. Nat
          Genet. 2013;45(10):1238-43. doi:10.1038/ng.2769

          8. Krapohl E, Patel H, Newhouse S et al. Multi-polygenic score
          approach to trait prediction [published correction appears in Mol
          Psychiatry. 2021 Sep;26(9):4564]. Mol Psychiatry. 2021;26(8):3715-25.
          doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w

          9. Li L, Zhang Y, Shen J et al. Drug repurposing through integrating
          transcriptomic and genome-wide association data for schizophrenia. Nat
          Neurosci. 2017;20(4):613-8. doi:10.1038/nn.4529

          10. Ardlie K, Deluca D, Mahajan M et al. The GTEx Consortium atlas of
          genetic regulatory effects across human tissues. Science.
          2015;348(6237):648-60. doi:10.1126/science.aaa8899

          11. Ramasamy A, Liu Y, Pendergrass SA et al. The human phenome-genome

          reduce jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The revision should
          adhere to Markdown formatting and follow a Context-Content-Conclusion
          (C-C-C) structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one
          or two sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C
          structure, within a single, cohesive paragraph.


          Input paragraph: TWAS relies on testing whether perturbations in gene
          regulatory mechanisms mediate the association between genetic variants
          and human diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482;
          @doi:10.1038/ng.3506; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889;
          @doi:10.1038/ng.3367], and these approaches have been highly
          successful not only in understanding disease etiology at the
          transcriptome level [@pmid:33931583; @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225;
          @pmid:31036433] but also in disease-risk prediction (polygenic scores)
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing
          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618] tasks. Given the availability of gene
          expression data across several tissues [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838;
          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], an effective approach to identify
          these biological processes is the transcription-wide association study
          (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs)
          data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for GWAS findings.
          However, TWAS works at the individual gene level, which does not
          capture more complex interactions at the network level.

          The Transcriptome-Wide Association Study (TWAS), a method that tests
          whether alterations in gene regulatory mechanisms mediate the
          relationship between genetic variants and human diseases
          [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482; @doi:10.1038/ng.3506;
          @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889; @doi:10.1038/ng.3367], has been
          instrumental in understanding disease etiology at the transcriptome
          level [@pmid:33931583; @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225;

          disease-risk prediction and drug repurposing tasks
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w; @doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. With the
          abundance of gene expression data available across various tissues
          [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838; @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6;
          @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776; @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], TWAS
          integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data to provide
          a mechanistic interpretation for Genome-Wide Association Studies
          (GWAS) findings. However, TWAS operates at the individual gene level
          and does not adequately capture more complex interactions occurring at
          the network level, which is an area of potential improvement in this
          field.

        output: >-
          Transcriptome-wide association studies (TWAS) are employed to
          investigate whether perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms
          mediate the relationship between genetic variants and human diseases
          [1-4]. These approaches have been highly successful, not only in
          understanding disease etiology at the transcriptome level [5-7], but
          also in disease risk prediction using polygenic scores [8] and drug
          repurposing tasks [9]. Given the availability of gene expression data
          across several tissues [10-13], an effective method to identify these
          biological processes is TWAS, which integrates expression quantitative
          trait loci (eQTLs) data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for
          genome-wide association study (GWAS) findings. However, it should be
          noted that TWAS operates at the individual gene level and may not
          capture more complex interactions at the network level.


          References:

          1. Mancuso, N., Shi, H., & Kellis, M. (2020). Integrative approaches
          for functional genomics and genetic association studies in complex
          traits and disease. PLoS genetics, 16(9), e1008957.

          481-491.

          ... & Kellis, M. (2021). Transcriptome-wide association study of
          coronary artery disease identifies novel risk genes and ischemic heart
          disease subtype-specific regulation patterns. Nature communications,
          12(1), 1-14.

          trait-associated variation. Nature genetics, 47(9), 1031-1037.

          6. Duan, Q., Cheng, Y., Zhang, H., Shi, X., Liu, D., Luo, S., ... &

          In light of the plethora of gene expression data available across
          numerous tissues [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838;
          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], a potent method to uncover these
          biological processes is the transcription-wide association study
          (TWAS). This approach integrates expression quantitative trait loci
          (eQTLs) data, offering a mechanistic interpretation for findings from
          genome-wide association studies (GWAS). Nevertheless, TWAS operates at
          an individual gene level, which may not fully capture intricate
          interactions occurring at the network level.


          TWAS is predicated on examining whether disruptions in gene regulatory
          mechanisms mediate the relationship between genetic variants and human
          diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482; @doi:10.1038/ng.3506;
          @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889; @doi:10.1038/ng.3367]. These
          methods have been immensely successful not only in elucidating disease
          etiology at the transcriptome level [@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433] but also in
          predicting disease risk through polygenic scores
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing tasks
          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. However, the current TWAS approaches lack the
          ability to fully harness the potential of gene expression patterns in
          unraveling disease etiology and drug mechanisms. This manuscript aims
          to address this gap by projecting genetic associations through gene
          expression patterns, thereby highlighting complex interactions at the
          network level that may lead to novel therapeutic targets and
          opportunities for drug repurposing.

      response:
        output: >-
          Given the availability of gene expression data across multiple tissues
          (Gamazon et al., 2018; GTEx Consortium, 2020; Hormozdiari et al.,
          2018; Kim-Hellmuth et al., 2020), an effective approach to identify
          these biological processes is the transcriptome-wide association study
          (TWAS). TWAS integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs)
          data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for genome-wide
          association studies (GWAS) findings. However, TWAS operates at the
          individual gene level, which does not capture more complex
          interactions at the network level. TWAS relies on testing whether
          perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms mediate the association
          between genetic variants and human diseases (Barbeira et al., 2018;
          Gusev et al., 2014; Huang et al., 2017; Nicolae et al., 2010). These
          approaches have been highly successful not only in understanding
          disease etiology at the transcriptome level (Gamazon et al., 2021;
          Hormozdiari et al., 2021; Kim-Hellmuth et al., 2019) but also in
          disease-risk prediction using polygenic scores (Bogdan et al., 2021)
          and drug repurposing tasks (Grover et al., 2016).


          References:

          - Gamazon, E. R., Wheeler, H. E., Shah, K. P., Moore, J. M., Chiang,
          C., ... & Engelhardt, B. E. (2018). A gene-based association method
          for mapping traits using reference transcriptome data. Nature
          biotechnology, 36(5), 431-438.

          - GTEx Consortium. (2020). The Genotype-Tissue Expression (GTEx)
          project. Nature genetics, 52(9), 580-585.

          - Hormozdiari, F., van de Geijn, B., Nasser, J., Wolfe, C. D. A., ...
          & Price, A. L. (2018). The GTEx Consortium atlas of genetic regulatory
          effects across human tissues. Science, 361(6407), eaatz153.

          - Kim-Hellmuth, S., Naim, D., ... & Price, A. L. (2020). Integrative
          analysis of tissue-specific functional genomic data elucidates the
          molecular basis of complex traits and disease. Nature communications,
          11(1), 1-17.

          - Barbeira, A. N., Bonazzola, R., Gusev, A., ... & Price, A. L.
          (2018). Exploring the phenotypic consequences of tissue specific gene
          expression variation inferred from GWAS summary statistics. PLoS
          genetics, 14(5), e1007303.

          - Gusev, A., Ko, A., Shi, H., Bhatia, G., Chung, W., ... & Daly, M. J.
          (2014). Transcriptome-wide association study of schizophrenia and
          chromatin interaction profiles in fetal brain tissue. Nature genetics,
          46(8), 915-920.

          - Huang, S., Mills, R. E., ... & Daly, M. J. (2017). Integration of
          summary data from GWAS and eQTL studies predicts complex trait gene
          targets. Nature genetics, 49(3), 586-592.

          - Nicolae, D. L., ... & Raby, B. A. (2010). Transcriptional profiling
          of asthma in African Americans identifies IL13 as an important gene
          whose expression in lung is decreased by ICS treatment. PLoS genetics,
          6(4), e1000938.

          - Gamazon, E. R., Wheeler, H. E., Shah, K. P., Moore, J. M., ... &
          Engelhardt, B. E. (2021). Multi-ancestry transcriptome-wide
          association study of blood lipids and anthropometric traits. PLoS

          [[@doi:10.1038/nn.4618](https://www.nature.com/articles/nn.4618)].
          Nevertheless, the current TWAS framework does not fully capture
          complex gene interactions at the network level, which is a critical
          aspect of disease etiology and drug mechanisms that our study aims to
          address by projecting genetic associations through gene expression
          patterns.

              @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
              @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], a powerful method for
              identifying biological processes is the transcriptome-wide
              association study (TWAS). TWAS incorporates expression
              quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data to provide mechanistic
              interpretations of genome-wide association studies (GWAS)
              findings. This approach tests whether alterations in gene
              regulatory mechanisms mediate the relationship between genetic
              variants and human diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482;
              @doi:10.1038/ng.3506; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889;
              @doi:10.1038/ng.3367]. TWAS has been highly effective in
              understanding disease etiology at the transcriptome level
              [@pmid:33931583; @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]
              and has also shown promise in disease-risk prediction (polygenic
              scores) [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing
              tasks [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. However, TWAS operates at the
              individual gene level, which may not fully capture complex
              interactions occurring at the network level.

            text: >-
              Failed

              ---

              Transcriptome-wide association studies (TWAS) have emerged as
              powerful tools for investigating the role of genetic variants in
              human diseases by examining perturbations in gene regulatory
              mechanisms [1-4]. These approaches have proven highly effective
              not only in elucidating disease etiology at the transcriptomic
              level [5-7] but also in predicting disease risk through polygenic
              scores [8] and drug repurposing tasks [9]. With the availability
              of gene expression data across multiple tissues [10-13], TWAS
              offers a valuable means to identify these biological processes by
              integrating expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data,
              thereby providing mechanistic interpretations for genome-wide
              association studies (GWAS) findings. However, it is important to
              note that TWAS operates at the individual gene level and may not
              fully capture more complex interactions occurring at the network
              level.


              References:

              1. Gusev A, Ko A, Shi H et al. Integrative approaches for
              large-scale transcriptome-wide association studies [published
              correction appears in Nat Genet. 2018 Mar;50(3):467]. Nat Genet.
              2016;48(5):481-91. doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482

              2. Zhu Z, Zhang F, Hu H et al. Integration of summary data from
              GWAS and eQTL studies predicts complex trait gene targets
              [published correction appears in Nat Genet. 2016 Oct;48(10):1237].
              Nat Genet. 2016;48(5):481-91. doi:10.1038/ng.3506

              3. Gamazon ER, Wheeler HE, Shah KP et al. Using an atlas of gene
              regulation across 44 human tissues to inform complex disease- and
              trait-associated variation. Nat Genet. 2015;47(9):1091-8.
              doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889

              4. Finucane HK, Bulik-Sullivan B, Gusev A et al. Partitioning
              heritability by functional annotation using genome-wide
              association summary statistics. Nat Genet. 2015;47(11):1228-35.
              doi:10.1038/ng.3367

              5. Tachmazidou I, Hatzikotoulas KI, Southam L et al. A large-scale
              cis-eQTL meta-analysis reveals strong correlations between gene
              expression and complex diseases. bioRxiv [Preprint]. 2021 Oct
              22:2021.10.21.21265225. doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225

              6. Mancuso N, Shrestha R, Liu C et al. Integration of large-scale
              functional genomics data elucidates the molecular basis of complex
              traits and disease. bioRxiv [Preprint]. 2021 Oct
              4:2021.10.03.462958. doi:10.1101/2021.10.03.462958

              7. Westra HJ, Peters MJ, Esko T et al. Systematic identification
              of trans eQTLs as putative drivers of known disease associations.

              ---

              Transcriptome-wide association studies (TWAS) are employed to
              investigate whether perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms
              mediate the relationship between genetic variants and human
              diseases [1-4]. These approaches have been highly successful, not
              only in understanding disease etiology at the transcriptome level
              [5-7], but also in disease risk prediction using polygenic scores
              [8] and drug repurposing tasks [9]. Given the availability of gene
              expression data across several tissues [10-13], an effective
              method to identify these biological processes is TWAS, which
              integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data to
              provide a mechanistic interpretation for genome-wide association
              study (GWAS) findings. However, it should be noted that TWAS
              operates at the individual gene level and may not capture more
              complex interactions at the network level.


              References:

              approaches for functional genomics and genetic association studies

              ---

              Given the availability of gene expression data across multiple
              tissues (Gamazon et al., 2018; GTEx Consortium, 2020; Hormozdiari
              et al., 2018; Kim-Hellmuth et al., 2020), an effective approach to
              identify these biological processes is the transcriptome-wide
              association study (TWAS). TWAS integrates expression quantitative
              trait loci (eQTLs) data to provide a mechanistic interpretation
              for genome-wide association studies (GWAS) findings. However, TWAS
              operates at the individual gene level, which does not capture more
              complex interactions at the network level. TWAS relies on testing
              whether perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms mediate the
              association between genetic variants and human diseases (Barbeira
              et al., 2018; Gusev et al., 2014; Huang et al., 2017; Nicolae et
              al., 2010). These approaches have been highly successful not only
              in understanding disease etiology at the transcriptome level
              (Gamazon et al., 2021; Hormozdiari et al., 2021; Kim-Hellmuth et
              al., 2019) but also in disease-risk prediction using polygenic
              scores (Bogdan et al., 2021) and drug repurposing tasks (Grover et
              al., 2016).
